<p><i><b>The Lion King</b></i> is a 1994 American animated <a href="/wiki/Epic_film" title="Epic film">epic</a> <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">musical</a> film, produced by <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios" title="Walt Disney Animation Studios">Walt Disney Feature Animation</a> and released by <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Walt Disney Pictures</a>. It is the 32nd <a href="/wiki/List_of_Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios_films" title="List of Walt Disney Animation Studios films">Disney animated feature film</a>. The story takes place in a kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lions" title="Lions">lions</a> in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> and was influenced by <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i>. The film was produced during a period known as the <a href="/wiki/Disney_Renaissance" title="Disney Renaissance">Disney Renaissance</a>. <i>The Lion King</i> was directed by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Allers" title="Roger Allers">Roger Allers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rob_Minkoff" title="Rob Minkoff">Rob Minkoff</a>, produced by <a href="/wiki/Don_Hahn" title="Don Hahn">Don Hahn</a>, and has a screenplay credited to <a href="/wiki/Irene_Mecchi" title="Irene Mecchi">Irene Mecchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Roberts_(writer)" title="Jonathan Roberts (writer)">Jonathan Roberts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Linda_Woolverton" title="Linda Woolverton">Linda Woolverton</a>. Its original songs were written by composer <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a> and lyricist <a href="/wiki/Tim_Rice" title="Tim Rice">Tim Rice</a>, and original scores were written by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Zimmer" title="Hans Zimmer">Hans Zimmer</a>. The film features an <a href="/wiki/Ensemble_cast" title="Ensemble cast">ensemble voice cast</a> that includes <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Broderick" title="Matthew Broderick">Matthew Broderick</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Earl_Jones" title="James Earl Jones">James Earl Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Irons" title="Jeremy Irons">Jeremy Irons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Taylor_Thomas" title="Jonathan Taylor Thomas">Jonathan Taylor Thomas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moira_Kelly" title="Moira Kelly">Moira Kelly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Lane" title="Nathan Lane">Nathan Lane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernie_Sabella" title="Ernie Sabella">Ernie Sabella</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rowan_Atkinson" title="Rowan Atkinson">Rowan Atkinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Guillaume" title="Robert Guillaume">Robert Guillaume</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madge_Sinclair" title="Madge Sinclair">Madge Sinclair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whoopi_Goldberg" title="Whoopi Goldberg">Whoopi Goldberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheech_Marin" title="Cheech Marin">Cheech Marin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Cummings" title="Jim Cummings">Jim Cummings</a>.</p>
<p><i>The Lion King</i> tells the story of <a href="/wiki/Simba" title="Simba">Simba</a>, a young lion who is to succeed his father, Mufasa, as King of the Pride Lands; however, after Simba's uncle Scar murders Mufasa, Simba is manipulated into thinking he was responsible and flees into <a href="/wiki/Exile" title="Exile">exile</a>. Upon maturation living with <a href="/wiki/Timon_and_Pumbaa" title="Timon and Pumbaa">two wastrels</a>, Simba is given some valuable perspective from his childhood friend, Nala, and his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaman" title="Shaman">shaman</a>, Rafiki, before returning to challenge Scar to end his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyranny" title="Tyranny">tyranny</a> and take his place in the Circle of Life as the rightful King.</p>
<p>Development of <i>The Lion King</i> began in 1988 during a meeting between <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Katzenberg" title="Jeffrey Katzenberg">Jeffrey Katzenberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_E._Disney" title="Roy E. Disney">Roy E. Disney</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Schneider_(film_executive)" title="Peter Schneider (film executive)">Peter Schneider</a> while promoting <i><a href="/wiki/Oliver_%26_Company" title="Oliver &amp; Company">Oliver &amp; Company</a></i> in Europe. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Disch" title="Thomas Disch">Thomas Disch</a> wrote a <a href="/wiki/Film_treatment" title="Film treatment">film treatment</a>, and Woolverton developed the first scripts while <a href="/wiki/George_Scribner" title="George Scribner">George Scribner</a> was signed on as director, being later joined by Allers. Production began in 1991 concurrently with <i><a href="/wiki/Pocahontas_(1995_film)" title="Pocahontas (1995 film)">Pocahontas</a></i>, which wound up attracting most of Disney's top animators. Some time after the staff traveled to <a href="/wiki/Hell%27s_Gate_National_Park" title="Hell's Gate National Park">Hell's Gate National Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> to research on the film's setting and animals, Scribner left production disagreeing with the decision to turn the film into a musical and was replaced by Minkoff. When Hahn joined the project, he was dissatisfied with the script and the story was promptly rewritten. Nearly 20 minutes of animation sequences were produced at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disney-MGM_Studios" title="Disney-MGM Studios">Disney-MGM Studios</a> in Florida. <a href="/wiki/Computer_animation" title="Computer animation">Computer animation</a> was also used in several scenes, most notably in the <a href="/wiki/Wildebeest" title="Wildebeest">wildebeest</a> stampede sequence.</p>
<p><i>The Lion King</i> was released on June 15, 1994, to a positive reaction from critics, who praised the film for its music, story, and animation; it finished its theatrical run as the highest-grossing release of 1994 and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest-grossing_films" title="List of highest-grossing films">second highest-grossing film of all time</a>. <i>The Lion King</i> garnered two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Awards</a> for its achievement in music and the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award" title="Golden Globe Award">Golden Globe Award</a> for <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award_for_Best_Motion_Picture_%E2%80%93_Musical_or_Comedy" title="Golden Globe Award for Best Motion Picture – Musical or Comedy">Best Motion Picture – Musical or Comedy</a>. The film has led to <a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King_(franchise)" title="The Lion King (franchise)">many derived works</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King_(musical)" title="The Lion King (musical)">a Broadway adaptation</a>; two <a href="/wiki/Direct-to-video" title="Direct-to-video">direct-to-video</a> follow-ups—the sequel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King_II:_Simba%27s_Pride" title="The Lion King II: Simba's Pride">The Lion King II: Simba's Pride</a></i> (1998), and the prequel/<a href="/wiki/Sequel#Parallel" title="Sequel">parallel</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King_1%C2%BD" title="The Lion King 1½">The Lion King 1½</a></i> (2004)—; two television series, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timon_and_Pumbaa_(TV_series)" title="Timon and Pumbaa (TV series)">Timon and Pumbaa</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_Guard" title="The Lion Guard">The Lion Guard</a></i>; and a <a href="/wiki/3D_film" title="3D film">3D</a> re-release, in 2011.</p>
<p>In 2016, the film was selected for preservation in the United States <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Registry" title="National Film Registry">National Film Registry</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> as being "culturally, historically, or aesthetically significant".</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plot">Plot</span></h2>
<p>In the Pride Lands of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, a lion rules over the animal kingdom from Pride Rock. King Mufasa and Queen Sarabi's newborn son, <a href="/wiki/Simba" title="Simba">Simba</a>, is presented to the assembled animals by Rafiki, a <a href="/wiki/Mandrill" title="Mandrill">mandrill</a> who serves as <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shaman</a> and advisor. Mufasa shows young Simba the Pride Lands and explains to him the responsibilities of kingship and the "circle of life" which connects all living things. Mufasa's younger brother, <a href="/wiki/Scar_(The_Lion_King)" title="Scar (The Lion King)">Scar</a>, covets the throne and plots to eliminate Mufasa and Simba so he may become king. He tricks Simba and his best friend Nala—to whom Simba is betrothed—into exploring a forbidden <a href="/wiki/Elephants%27_graveyard" title="Elephants' graveyard">elephants' graveyard</a>, where they are attacked by three <a href="/wiki/Spotted_hyena" title="Spotted hyena">spotted hyenas</a> who are in league with Scar. Mufasa is alerted to the danger by his <a href="/wiki/Majordomo" title="Majordomo">majordomo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hornbill" title="Hornbill">hornbill</a> Zazu, and rescues the cubs. Though angry with Simba, Mufasa forgives him and explains that the great kings of the past watch over them from the night sky, from which he will one day watch over Simba.</p>
<p>Scar sets a trap for his brother and nephew, luring Simba into a gorge and having the hyenas drive a large herd of <a href="/wiki/Wildebeest" title="Wildebeest">wildebeest</a> into a stampede that will trample him. He informs Mufasa of Simba's peril, knowing the king will rush to save his son. Mufasa saves Simba but ends up hanging perilously from the gorge's cliffside and is betrayed by Scar, who pushes him off the cliff to his death. Scar convinces Simba that the tragedy was Simba's own fault and advises him to flee the kingdom. He orders the hyenas to kill the cub, but Simba escapes. Scar tells the pride that both Mufasa and Simba were killed in the stampede and steps forward as the new king, allowing a large pack of hyenas to live in the Pride Lands.</p>
<p>Simba collapses in a desert and is rescued by <a href="/wiki/Timon_and_Pumbaa" title="Timon and Pumbaa">Timon and Pumbaa</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Meerkat" title="Meerkat">meerkat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_warthog" title="Common warthog">warthog</a> who are fellow outcasts. Simba grows up in the jungle with his two new friends, living a carefree life under the motto "<a href="/wiki/Hakuna_matata" title="Hakuna matata">hakuna matata</a>" ("no worries" in <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>). Now a young adult, Simba rescues Timon and Pumbaa from a hungry lioness who turns out to be Nala. She and Simba reunite and fall in love, and she urges him to return home, telling him the Pride Lands have become a drought-stricken wasteland under Scar's reign. Feeling guilty over his father's death, Simba refuses and storms off. He encounters Rafiki, who tells him that Mufasa's spirit lives on in Simba. Simba is visited by the ghost of Mufasa in the night sky, who tells him he must take his rightful place as king. Realizing he can no longer run from his past, Simba decides to return home.</p>
<p>Aided by his friends, Simba sneaks past the hyenas at Pride Rock and confronts Scar after he strikes Sarabi. Scar taunts Simba over his role in Mufasa's death and backs him to the edge of the rock, where he reveals to him that he murdered Mufasa. Enraged, Simba pins Scar to the ground and forces him to reveal the truth to the rest of the pride. Timon, Pumbaa, Rafiki, Zazu, and the lionesses fend off the hyenas while Scar, attempting to escape, is cornered by Simba at the top of Pride Rock. Scar begs for mercy and attempts to blame the hyenas for his actions; Simba spares his life but orders him to leave the Pride Lands forever. Scar attacks his nephew, but Simba manages to toss him from the top of the rock. Scar survives the fall but is killed by the hyenas, who overheard him betray them to Simba. With his enemies gone, Simba takes over the kingship as the rains begin to fall, restoring life to the land.</p>
<p>Later, with Pride Rock restored to its former glory, Rafiki presents Simba and Nala's newborn cub to the assembled animals, continuing the circle of life.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Voice_cast">Voice cast</span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/Matthew_Broderick" title="Matthew Broderick">Matthew Broderick</a> as <a href="/wiki/Simba" title="Simba">Simba</a>, son of Mufasa and Sarabi, who grows up to become King of the Pride Lands. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Williams_(musician)" title="Joseph Williams (musician)">Joseph Williams</a> provided adult Simba's singing voice. <a href="/wiki/Mark_Henn" title="Mark Henn">Mark Henn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruben_A._Aquino" title="Ruben A. Aquino">Ruben A. Aquino</a> respectively served as the supervising animators for young and adult Simba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup>

<li><a href="/wiki/James_Earl_Jones" title="James Earl Jones">James Earl Jones</a> as Mufasa, Simba's father, King of the Pride Lands as the film begins. Tony Fucile served as the supervising animator for Mufasa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Moira_Kelly" title="Moira Kelly">Moira Kelly</a> as <a href="/wiki/Nala_(The_Lion_King)" title="Nala (The Lion King)">Nala</a>, Simba's best friend and later his wife. <a href="/wiki/Sally_Dworsky" title="Sally Dworsky">Sally Dworsky</a> provided her singing voice. Aaron Blaise and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthony_de_Rosa" title="Anthony de Rosa">Anthony de Rosa</a> respectively served as the supervising animators for young and adult Nala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li>Niketa Calame provided the voice of young Nala while Laura Williams provided her singing voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nathan_Lane" title="Nathan Lane">Nathan Lane</a> as <a href="/wiki/Timon_and_Pumbaa" title="Timon and Pumbaa">Timon</a>, a wise-cracking and self-absorbed yet somewhat loyal <a href="/wiki/Meerkat" title="Meerkat">meerkat</a> who becomes one of Simba's best friends and adoptive parents. Michael Surrey served as his supervising animator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ernie_Sabella" title="Ernie Sabella">Ernie Sabella</a> as <a href="/wiki/Timon_and_Pumbaa#Appearances" title="Timon and Pumbaa">Pumbaa</a>, a naïve <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warthog" title="Warthog">warthog</a> who suffers from flatulence and is Timon's best friend and also becomes one of Simba's best friends and adoptive parents. <a href="/wiki/Tony_Bancroft" title="Tony Bancroft">Tony Bancroft</a> served as his supervising animator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Guillaume" title="Robert Guillaume">Robert Guillaume</a> as Rafiki, a wise old <a href="/wiki/Mandrill" title="Mandrill">mandrill</a> (although, while counseling Simba, he jokes that "you are a <a href="/wiki/Baboon" title="Baboon">baboon</a>, and I am not") who serves as shaman of the Pride Lands and presents newborn cubs of the King and Queen to the animals of the Pride Lands. <a href="/wiki/James_Baxter_(animator)" title="James Baxter (animator)">James Baxter</a> served as the supervising animator for Rafiki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rowan_Atkinson" title="Rowan Atkinson">Rowan Atkinson</a> as Zazu, a <a href="/wiki/Hornbill" title="Hornbill">hornbill</a> who serves as the king's <a href="/wiki/Majordomo" title="Majordomo">majordomo</a> (or "Mufasa's little stooge", as Shenzi calls him). <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Woodbury" title="Ellen Woodbury">Ellen Woodbury</a> served as the supervising animator for Zazu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Madge_Sinclair" title="Madge Sinclair">Madge Sinclair</a> as Sarabi, Mufasa's mate, Simba's mother, and the leader of the lioness hunting party. <a href="/wiki/Russ_Edmonds" title="Russ Edmonds">Russ Edmonds</a> served as the supervising animator for Sarabi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li>The three <a href="/wiki/Spotted_hyena" title="Spotted hyena">hyenas</a> who serve Scar were animated by Alex Kupershmidt and David Burgess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Whoopi_Goldberg" title="Whoopi Goldberg">Whoopi Goldberg</a> as Shenzi, the sassy and short-tempered female leader of the trio.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cheech_Marin" title="Cheech Marin">Cheech Marin</a> as Banzai, an aggressive and hot-headed hyena prone to complaining and acting on impulse.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jim_Cummings" title="Jim Cummings">Jim Cummings</a> as Ed, a dim-witted hyena who does not talk, only communicating through <a href="/wiki/Spotted_hyena#Vocalizations" title="Spotted hyena">laughter</a>. Cummings also voiced a <a href="/wiki/Gopher" title="Gopher">gopher</a> that talks with Zazu and replaced Irons as Scar in certain lines of "<a href="/wiki/Be_Prepared_(song)" title="Be Prepared (song)">Be Prepared</a>" after Irons blew his voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Zoe Leader as Sarafina, Nala's mother, who is shown briefly talking to Simba's mother, Sarabi.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h3>
<p>The idea for <i>The Lion King</i> was conceived in late 1988 during a conversation between <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Katzenberg" title="Jeffrey Katzenberg">Jeffrey Katzenberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_E._Disney" title="Roy E. Disney">Roy E. Disney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Schneider_(film_executive)" title="Peter Schneider (film executive)">Peter Schneider</a> on a plane to Europe to promote <i><a href="/wiki/Oliver_%26_Company" title="Oliver &amp; Company">Oliver &amp; Company</a></i>. During the conversation, the topic of a story set in Africa came up, and Katzenberg immediately jumped at the idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-memoir_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-memoir-5">[5]</a></sup> The idea was then developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Feature_Animation" title="Walt Disney Feature Animation">Walt Disney Feature Animation</a>'s vice president for creative affairs Charlie Fink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neuwirth_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-neuwirth-6">[6]</a></sup> Katzenberg decided to add elements involving coming of age and death, and ideas from personal life experiences, such as some of his trials in his bumpy road in politics, saying about the film, "It is a little bit about myself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pride_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-pride-7">[7]</a></sup> In November of that year <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Disch" title="Thomas Disch">Thomas Disch</a> (author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brave_Little_Toaster_(novel)" title="The Brave Little Toaster (novel)">The Brave Little Toaster</a></i>) wrote a <a href="/wiki/Film_treatment" title="Film treatment">treatment</a> entitled <i>King of the Kalahari</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingDisch_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingDisch-8">[8]</a></sup> and afterwards, <a href="/wiki/Linda_Woolverton" title="Linda Woolverton">Linda Woolverton</a> spent a year writing drafts of the script, which was titled <i>King of the Beasts</i> and then <i>King of the Jungle</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neuwirth_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-neuwirth-6">[6]</a></sup> The original version of the film was very different from the final film. The plot was centered in a battle being between lions and baboons with Scar being the leader of the baboons, Rafiki being a cheetah,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pride_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-pride-7">[7]</a></sup> and Timon and Pumbaa being Simba's childhood friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dvdcommentary_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-dvdcommentary-9">[9]</a></sup> Simba would also not leave the kingdom but become a "lazy, slovenly, horrible character" due to manipulations from Scar, so Simba could be overthrown after coming of age. By 1990, producer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Schumacher" title="Thomas Schumacher">Thomas Schumacher</a>, who had just completed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rescuers_Down_Under" title="The Rescuers Down Under">The Rescuers Down Under</a></i>, decided to attach himself to the project "because lions are cool".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neuwirth_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-neuwirth-6">[6]</a></sup> Schumacher likened the script for <i>King of the Jungle</i> to "an animated <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Channel" title="National Geographic Channel">National Geographic</a></i> special".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schu_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-schu-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Oliver &amp; Company</i> director <a href="/wiki/George_Scribner" title="George Scribner">George Scribner</a> was the initial director of the film,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mane_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-mane-11">[11]</a></sup> being later joined by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Allers" title="Roger Allers">Roger Allers</a>, who was the lead story man on <i><a href="/wiki/Beauty_and_the_Beast_(1991_film)" title="Beauty and the Beast (1991 film)">Beauty and the Beast</a></i> in October 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-memoir_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-memoir-5">[5]</a></sup> Allers brought with him <a href="/wiki/Brenda_Chapman" title="Brenda Chapman">Brenda Chapman</a>, who would become the head of story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neuwirth_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-neuwirth-6">[6]</a></sup> Afterwards, several of the lead crew members, including Allers, Scribner, Hahn, Chapman, and production designer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chris_Sanders_(director)" title="Chris Sanders (director)">Chris Sanders</a>, took a trip to <a href="/wiki/Hell%27s_Gate_National_Park" title="Hell's Gate National Park">Hell's Gate National Park</a> in Kenya, in order to study and gain an appreciation of the environment for the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-art_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-art-12">[12]</a></sup> After six months of story development work Scribner decided to leave the project, as he clashed with Allers and the producers on their decision to turn the film into a musical, as Scribner's intention was of making a documentary-like film more focused on natural aspects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-memoir_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-memoir-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mattinson_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-mattinson-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rob_Minkoff" title="Rob Minkoff">Rob Minkoff</a> replaced Scribner,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-art_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-art-12">[12]</a></sup> and producer <a href="/wiki/Don_Hahn" title="Don Hahn">Don Hahn</a> joined the production as Schumacher became only an executive producer due to Disney promoting him to Vice President of Development for Feature Animation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schu_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-schu-10">[10]</a></sup> Hahn found the script unfocused and lacking a clear theme, and after establishing the main theme as "leaving childhood and facing up to the realities of the world", asked for a final retool. Allers, Minkoff, Chapman and Hahn then rewrote the story across two weeks of meetings with directors <a href="/wiki/Kirk_Wise" title="Kirk Wise">Kirk Wise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gary_Trousdale" title="Gary Trousdale">Gary Trousdale</a>, who had just finished <i>Beauty and the Beast</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-art_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-art-12">[12]</a></sup> The script also had its title changed from <i>King of the Jungle</i> to <i>The Lion King</i>, as the setting was not the jungle but the savannah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-memoir_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-memoir-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Lion King</i> was the first Disney animated feature to be an original story, rather than be based on an already existing work. The filmmakers have said that the story of <i>The Lion King</i> was inspired by the lives of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_(patriarch)" title="Joseph (patriarch)">Joseph</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>, from the Bible, and <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlatinumEditionDVDOrigins_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlatinumEditionDVDOrigins-14">[14]</a></sup> During the summer of 1992, the team was joined by screenwriter <a href="/wiki/Irene_Mecchi" title="Irene Mecchi">Irene Mecchi</a>, with a second screenwriter, <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Roberts_(writer)" title="Jonathan Roberts (writer)">Jonathan Roberts</a>, joining a few months later. Mecchi and Roberts took charge of the revision process, fixing unresolved emotional issues in the script and adding comic business for Pumbaa, Timon, and the hyenas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup> Lyricist <a href="/wiki/Tim_Rice" title="Tim Rice">Tim Rice</a> worked closely with the writing team, flying to California at least once a month because his songs needed to work in the narrative continuity. Rice's lyrics—which were reworked up to the production's end—were pinned to the <a href="/wiki/Storyboard" title="Storyboard">storyboards</a> during development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-art_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-art-12">[12]</a></sup> Rewrites were frequent, with animator <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Deja" title="Andreas Deja">Andreas Deja</a> saying that completed scenes would be delivered, only for the response to be that parts needed to be reanimated because of dialog changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neuwirth_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-neuwirth-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Casting">Casting</span></h3>
<p>The voice actors were chosen for how they fit and could add to the characters—for instance, <a href="/wiki/James_Earl_Jones" title="James Earl Jones">James Earl Jones</a> was cast because the directors found his voice "powerful" and similar to a lion's roar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-making_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-making-15">[15]</a></sup> Jones commented that during the years of production, Mufasa "became more and more of a dopey dad instead of [a] grand king".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fone_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-fone-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nathan_Lane" title="Nathan Lane">Nathan Lane</a> originally auditioned for Zazu, and <a href="/wiki/Ernie_Sabella" title="Ernie Sabella">Ernie Sabella</a>, for one of the hyenas. Upon meeting each other at the recording studio, the actors, who at the time both co-starred in <i><a href="/wiki/Guys_and_Dolls" title="Guys and Dolls">Guys and Dolls</a></i>, were asked to record together as hyenas. The directors laughed at their performance and decided to cast them as Timon and Pumbaa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-making_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-making-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latimes_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-latimes-17">[17]</a></sup> For the hyenas, the original intention was to reunite <a href="/wiki/Cheech_%26_Chong" title="Cheech &amp; Chong">Cheech &amp; Chong</a>, but while <a href="/wiki/Cheech_Marin" title="Cheech Marin">Cheech Marin</a> accepted to play Banzai, <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Chong" title="Tommy Chong">Tommy Chong</a> was unavailable. Thus his role was changed into a female hyena, Shenzi, who was voiced by <a href="/wiki/Whoopi_Goldberg" title="Whoopi Goldberg">Whoopi Goldberg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dvdcommentary_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-dvdcommentary-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Matthew Broderick was cast as adult Simba early during production, and during the three years of voice acting only recorded with another actor once, and only discovered Moira Kelly voiced Nala at the premiere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Jeremy Irons had at first refused the role due to not being comfortable going from the dramatic performance as <a href="/wiki/Claus_von_B%C3%BClow" title="Claus von Bülow">Claus von Bülow</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Reversal_of_Fortune" title="Reversal of Fortune">Reversal of Fortune</a></i> to a comedic role. But once he came in, Irons' performance even inspired the writers to incorporate more of his acting as von Bülow—adding one of that character's lines, "You have no idea"—and animator Andreas Deja to watch both <i>Reversal of Fortune</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Damage_(1992_film)" title="Damage (1992 film)">Damage</a></i> to pick up Irons's facial traits and tics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fone_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-fone-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animation">Animation</span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:33%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"<i>The Lion King</i> was considered a little movie because we were going to take some risks. The pitch for the story was a lion cub gets framed for murder by his uncle set to the music of Elton John. People said, 'What? Good luck with that.' But for some reason, the people who ended up on the movie were highly passionate about it and motivated."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Don Hahn<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latimes_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-latimes-17">[17]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>The development of <i>The Lion King</i> coincided with that of <i><a href="/wiki/Pocahontas_(1995_film)" title="Pocahontas (1995 film)">Pocahontas</a></i>, which most of the animators of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Feature_Animation" title="Walt Disney Feature Animation">Walt Disney Feature Animation</a> decided to work on instead, believing it would be the more prestigious and successful of the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlatinumEditionDVDOrigins_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-PlatinumEditionDVDOrigins-14">[14]</a></sup> The story artists also did not have much faith in the project, with Chapman declaring she was reluctant to accept the job "because the story wasn't very good",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neuwirth_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-neuwirth-6">[6]</a></sup> and writer <a href="/wiki/Burny_Mattinson" title="Burny Mattinson">Burny Mattinson</a> saying to co-worker <a href="/wiki/Joe_Ranft" title="Joe Ranft">Joe Ranft</a> "I don't know who is going to want to watch that one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mattinson_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-mattinson-13">[13]</a></sup> Most of the leading animators either were doing their first major work supervising a character, or had much interest in animating an animal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pride_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-pride-7">[7]</a></sup> Thirteen of these supervising animators, both in California and in Florida, were responsible for establishing the personalities and setting the tone for the film's main characters. The animation leads for the main characters included <a href="/wiki/Mark_Henn" title="Mark Henn">Mark Henn</a> on young Simba, <a href="/wiki/Ruben_A._Aquino" title="Ruben A. Aquino">Ruben A. Aquino</a> on adult Simba, <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Deja" title="Andreas Deja">Andreas Deja</a> on Scar, Aaron Blaise on young Nala, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_DeRosa" title="Anthony DeRosa">Anthony DeRosa</a> on adult Nala, and Tony Fucile on Mufasa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-13"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup> Nearly 20 minutes of the film, including the "I Just Can't Wait to Be King" sequence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dvdcommentary_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-dvdcommentary-9">[9]</a></sup> were animated at the <a href="/wiki/Disney%27s_Hollywood_Studios" title="Disney's Hollywood Studios">Disney-MGM Studios</a> facility. More than 600 artists, animators, and technicians contributed to <i>The Lion King</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mane_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-mane-11">[11]</a></sup> Weeks before the film was to be released, the <a href="/wiki/1994_Northridge_earthquake" title="1994 Northridge earthquake">1994 Northridge earthquake</a> shut down the studio and required the animators to finish their work from home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The character animators studied real-life animals for reference, as was done for the 1942 Disney film <i><a href="/wiki/Bambi" title="Bambi">Bambi</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Jim_Fowler" title="Jim Fowler">Jim Fowler</a>, renowned wildlife expert, visited the studios on several occasions with an assortment of lions and other savannah inhabitants to discuss behavior and help the animators give their drawings authenticity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-art_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-art-12">[12]</a></sup> The animators also studied animal movements at the <a href="/wiki/Zoo_Miami" title="Zoo Miami">Miami MetroZoo</a> under guidance from wildlife expert <a href="/wiki/Ron_Magill" title="Ron Magill">Ron Magill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The Pride Lands are modeled on the Kenyan national park visited by the crew. Varied focal lengths and lenses were employed to differ from the habitual portrayal of Africa in documentaries—which employ <a href="/wiki/Telephoto_lens" title="Telephoto lens">telephoto lenses</a> to shoot the wildlife from a distance. The epic feel drew inspiration from concept studies by artist Hans Bacher—which, following Scribner's request for realism, tried to depict effects such as <a href="/wiki/Lens_flare" title="Lens flare">lens flare</a>—and the works of painters <a href="/wiki/Charles_Marion_Russell" title="Charles Marion Russell">Charles Marion Russell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Remington" title="Frederic Remington">Frederic Remington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maxfield_Parrish" title="Maxfield Parrish">Maxfield Parrish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-art_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-art-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Because the characters were not anthropomorphized, all the animators had to learn to draw four-legged animals, and the story and character development was done through use of longer shots following the characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dvdcommentary_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-dvdcommentary-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Computers helped the filmmakers present their vision in new ways. For the "wildebeest stampede" sequence, several distinct wildebeest characters were created in a 3D computer program, multiplied into hundreds, <a href="/wiki/Cel_shading" title="Cel shading">cel shaded</a> to look like drawn animation, and given randomized paths down a mountainside to simulate the real, unpredictable movement of a herd.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Five specially trained animators and technicians spent more than two years creating the two-and-a-half-minute stampede.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LionKingProduction_3-14"><a href="#cite_note-LionKingProduction-3">[3]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Computer_Animation_Production_System" title="Computer Animation Production System">CAPS</a> helped simulate camera movements such as <a href="/wiki/Tracking_shot" title="Tracking shot">tracking shots</a>, and was employed in coloring, lighting, and particle effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dvdcommentary_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-dvdcommentary-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King_(soundtrack)" title="The Lion King (soundtrack)">The Lion King (soundtrack)</a></div>
<p>Lyricist <a href="/wiki/Tim_Rice" title="Tim Rice">Tim Rice</a>, who was working with composer <a href="/wiki/Alan_Menken" title="Alan Menken">Alan Menken</a> on songs for <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_(1992_Disney_film)" title="Aladdin (1992 Disney film)">Aladdin</a></i>, was invited to write the songs, and accepted on the condition of finding a composing partner. As Menken was unavailable, the producers accepted Rice's suggestion of <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-making_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-making-15">[15]</a></sup> after Rice's invitation of <a href="/wiki/ABBA" title="ABBA">ABBA</a> fell through due to <a href="/wiki/Benny_Andersson" title="Benny Andersson">Benny Andersson</a> being busy with the musical <i><a href="/wiki/Kristina_fr%C3%A5n_Duvem%C3%A5la" title="Kristina från Duvemåla">Kristina från Duvemåla</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pride_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-pride-7">[7]</a></sup> John expressed an interest in writing "ultra-pop songs that kids would like; then adults can go and see those movies and get just as much pleasure out of them", mentioning a possible influence of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jungle_Book_(1967_film)" title="The Jungle Book (1967 film)">The Jungle Book</a></i>, where he felt the "music was so funny and appealed to kids and adults".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>John and Rice wrote five original songs for the film ("<a href="/wiki/Circle_of_Life" title="Circle of Life">Circle of Life</a>", "<a href="/wiki/I_Just_Can%27t_Wait_to_Be_King" title="I Just Can't Wait to Be King">I Just Can't Wait to Be King</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Be_Prepared_(song)" title="Be Prepared (song)">Be Prepared</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Hakuna_Matata_(song)" title="Hakuna Matata (song)">Hakuna Matata</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Can_You_Feel_the_Love_Tonight" title="Can You Feel the Love Tonight">Can You Feel the Love Tonight</a>"), with John's performance of "Can You Feel the Love Tonight" playing over the end credits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-music_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-music-25">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/IMAX" title="IMAX">IMAX</a> and DVD releases added another song, "The Morning Report", based on a song discarded during development that eventually featured in the <a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King_(musical)" title="The Lion King (musical)">live musical version of <i>The Lion King</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The score was composed by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Zimmer" title="Hans Zimmer">Hans Zimmer</a>, who was hired based on his work in two films in African settings, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Power_of_One_(film)" title="The Power of One (film)">The Power of One</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/A_World_Apart_(film)" title="A World Apart (film)">A World Apart</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-art_12-6"><a href="#cite_note-art-12">[12]</a></sup> and supplemented the score with traditional African music and choir elements arranged by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lebo_M" title="Lebo M">Lebo M</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-music_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-music-25">[25]</a></sup> Zimmer's partners <a href="/wiki/Mark_Mancina" title="Mark Mancina">Mark Mancina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jay_Rifkin" title="Jay Rifkin">Jay Rifkin</a> helped with arrangements and song production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Lion King</i> original motion picture soundtrack was released by <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Records" title="Walt Disney Records">Walt Disney Records</a> on July 13, 1994. It was the fourth-best-selling album of the year on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a> and the top-selling soundtrack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> It is the only soundtrack for an animated film to be <a href="/wiki/RIAA_certification" title="RIAA certification">certified Diamond</a> (10× platinum) by the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a>. Zimmer's complete instrumental score for the film was never originally given a full release by Disney, until the soundtrack's commemorative <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Records:_The_Legacy_Collection#The_Lion_King" title="Walt Disney Records: The Legacy Collection">20th anniversary re-release</a> in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> <i>The Lion King</i> also inspired the 1995 release <i><a href="/wiki/Rhythm_of_the_Pride_Lands" title="Rhythm of the Pride Lands">Rhythm of the Pride Lands</a></i>, with eight songs by Zimmer, Mancina, and Lebo M.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The use of the song "<a href="/wiki/The_Lion_Sleeps_Tonight" title="The Lion Sleeps Tonight">The Lion Sleeps Tonight</a>" in a scene with Timon and Pumbaa led to disputes between Disney and the family of South African <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Linda" title="Solomon Linda">Solomon Linda</a>, who composed the song (originally titled "Mbube") in 1939. In July 2004, Linda's filed suit, seeking $1.6 million in royalties from Disney. In February 2006, Linda's heirs reached a legal settlement with Abilene Music, who held the worldwide rights and had licensed the song to Disney for an undisclosed amount of money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-undisclosed_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-undisclosed-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span></h2>
<p>For <i>The Lion King</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_trailer" title="Film trailer">film trailer</a>, Disney opted to feature a single scene, the entire opening sequence with the song "Circle of Life". <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buena_Vista_Pictures_Distribution" title="Buena Vista Pictures Distribution">Buena Vista Pictures Distribution</a> president <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cook" title="Dick Cook">Dick Cook</a> said the decision was made for such an approach because "we were all so taken by the beauty and majesty of this piece that we felt like it was probably one of the best four minutes of film that we've seen", and Don Hahn added that "Circle of Life" worked as a trailer as it "came off so strong, and so good, and ended with such a bang". The trailer was released in November 1993, accompanying <i><a href="/wiki/The_Three_Musketeers_(1993_film)" title="The Three Musketeers (1993 film)">The Three Musketeers</a></i> in theaters, as only a third of <i>The Lion King</i> had been completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-den_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-den-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Audience reaction was enthusiastic, causing Hahn to have some initial concerns as he became afraid of not living up to the expectations raised by the preview.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-den_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-den-32">[32]</a></sup> Prior to the film's release, Disney did 11 <a href="/wiki/Test_screening" title="Test screening">test screenings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70mm_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-70mm-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Upon release, <i>The Lion King</i> was accompanied by an extensive marketing campaign which included tie-ins with <a href="/wiki/Burger_King" title="Burger King">Burger King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mattel" title="Mattel">Mattel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kodak" title="Kodak">Kodak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nestl%C3%A9" title="Nestlé">Nestlé</a> and <a href="/wiki/Payless_ShoeSource" title="Payless ShoeSource">Payless ShoeSource</a>, and various merchandise,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> accounting 186 licensed products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In 1994, Disney earned approximately $1 billion with products based on the film,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> with $214 million for <i>Lion King</i> toys during Christmas 1994 alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_media">Home media</span></h3>
<p><i>The Lion King</i> was first released on VHS and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laserdisc" title="Laserdisc">laserdisc</a> in the United States on March 3, 1995, under <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">Disney</a>'s "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Masterpiece_Collection" title="Walt Disney Masterpiece Collection">Masterpiece Collection</a>" video series. The VHS tape contained a special preview for <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Walt Disney Pictures</a>' then-upcoming <a href="/wiki/Animation" title="Animation">animated</a> <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Pocahontas_(1995_film)" title="Pocahontas (1995 film)">Pocahontas</a></i>, in which the title character (voiced by <a href="/wiki/Judy_Kuhn" title="Judy Kuhn">Judy Kuhn</a>) sings the musical number "<a href="/wiki/Colors_of_the_Wind" title="Colors of the Wind">Colors of the Wind</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In addition, Deluxe Editions of both formats were released. The VHS Deluxe Edition included the film, an exclusive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithograph" title="Lithograph">lithograph</a> of Rafiki and Simba (in some editions), a commemorative "Circle of Life" <a href="/wiki/Epigraph_(literature)" title="Epigraph (literature)">epigraph</a>, six concept art lithographs, another tape with the half-hour TV show <i>The Making of The Lion King</i>, and a certificate of authenticity. The CAV laserdisc Deluxe Edition also contained the film, six concept art lithographs and <i>The Making of The Lion King</i>, and added storyboards, character design artwork, concept art, rough animation, and a directors' commentary that the VHS edition did not have, on a total of four double sided discs. The VHS tape quickly became the best-selling videotape of all time: 4.5 million tapes were sold on the first day<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homevideosellingfigures_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-homevideosellingfigures-41">[41]</a></sup> and ultimately sales totaled more than 30 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWlionking_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWlionking-42">[42]</a></sup> before these home video versions went into <a href="/wiki/Moratorium_(entertainment)" title="Moratorium (entertainment)">moratorium</a> in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homevideoinfo_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-homevideoinfo-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 7, 2003, the film was re-released on VHS and released on DVD for the first time, titled <i>The Lion King: Platinum Edition</i>, as part of Disney's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platinum_Edition" title="Platinum Edition">Platinum Edition</a> line of animated classic DVDs. The DVD release featured two versions of the film on the first disc, a remastered version created for the 2002 <a href="/wiki/IMAX" title="IMAX">IMAX</a> release and an edited version of the IMAX release purporting to be the original 1994 theatrical version.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dvddifferences_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-dvddifferences-44">[44]</a></sup> A second disc, with bonus features, was also included in the DVD release. The film's soundtrack was provided both in its original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dolby_5.1" title="Dolby 5.1">Dolby 5.1</a> track and in a new Disney Enhanced Home Theater Mix, making this one of the first Disney DVDs so equipped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DVDfeatures_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-DVDfeatures-45">[45]</a></sup> By means of <a href="/wiki/Seamless_branching" title="Seamless branching">seamless branching</a>, the film could be viewed either with or without a newly created scene – a short conversation in the film replaced with a complete song ("The Morning Report"). A Special Collector's Gift Set was also released, containing the DVD set, five exclusive lithographed character portraits (new sketches created and signed by the original character animators), and an introductory book entitled <i>The Journey</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homevideoinfo_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-homevideoinfo-43">[43]</a></sup> The Platinum Edition of <i>The Lion King</i> featured changes made to the film during its IMAX re-release, including re-drawn <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a> in the "I Just Can't Wait to Be King" sequence as well as other alterations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dvddifferences_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-dvddifferences-44">[44]</a></sup> More than two million copies of the Platinum Edition DVD and VHS units were sold on the first day of release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homevideosellingfigures_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-homevideosellingfigures-41">[41]</a></sup> A DVD boxed set of the three <i>The Lion King</i> films (in two-disc Special Edition formats) was released on December 6, 2004. In January 2005, the film, along with the sequels, went back into moratorium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-outofprint_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-outofprint-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_Home_Entertainment" title="Walt Disney Studios Home Entertainment">Walt Disney Studios Home Entertainment</a> released the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Platinum_and_Diamond_Editions" title="Walt Disney Platinum and Diamond Editions">Diamond Edition</a> of <i>The Lion King</i> on October 4, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR_News_Wire_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-PR_News_Wire-47">[47]</a></sup> This marks the time that the film has been released in high-definition <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray" title="Blu-ray">Blu-ray</a> and on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_3D" title="Blu-ray 3D">Blu-ray 3D</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR_News_Wire_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-PR_News_Wire-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IGN_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-IGN-48">[48]</a></sup> The initial release was produced in three different packages: a two-disc version with Blu-ray and DVD; a four-disc version with Blu-ray, DVD, Blu-ray 3D, and <a href="/wiki/Digital_copy" title="Digital copy">digital copy</a>; and an eight-disc <a href="/wiki/Box_set" title="Box set">box set</a> that also includes the sequels <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Lion_King_2:_Simba%27s_Pride" title="The Lion King 2: Simba's Pride">The Lion King 2: Simba's Pride</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King_1%C2%BD" title="The Lion King 1½">The Lion King 1½</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR_News_Wire_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-PR_News_Wire-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IGN_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-IGN-48">[48]</a></sup> A standalone single-disc DVD release also followed on November 15, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR_News_Wire_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-PR_News_Wire-47">[47]</a></sup> The Diamond Edition topped the Blu-ray charts with over 1.5 million copies sold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The film sold 3.83 million Blu-ray units in total, leading to a $101.14 million income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Box_office">Box office</span></h3>
<p><i>The Lion King</i> earned $422,783,777 in North America and an $545,700,000 in other territories for a worldwide total of $968,483,777.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mojo_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-mojo-2">[2]</a></sup> It is currently the <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest-grossing_films" title="List of highest-grossing films">29th highest-grossing film</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest-grossing_animated_films#Worldwide_highest-grossing_animated_films" title="List of highest-grossing animated films">sixth highest-grossing animated film</a> of all time worldwide and the third highest-grossing film of <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios" title="Walt Disney Animation Studios">Walt Disney Animation Studios</a> (behind <i><a href="/wiki/Frozen_(2013_film)" title="Frozen (2013 film)">Frozen</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Zootopia" title="Zootopia">Zootopia</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The film was also the highest-grossing motion picture of <a href="/wiki/1994_in_film" title="1994 in film">1994 worldwide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> After its initial run, having earned $763.4 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lion-initial_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-lion-initial-54">[54]</a></sup> it ranked as the second-highest-grossing film of all time worldwide, behind <i><a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park_(film)" title="Jurassic Park (film)">Jurassic Park</a></i> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> It held the record for the highest-grossing animated feature film (in North America, outside North America, and worldwide) until it was surpassed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_animated" title="Computer animated">computer animated</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Finding_Nemo" title="Finding Nemo">Finding Nemo</a></i> (2003), <i><a href="/wiki/Shrek_2" title="Shrek 2">Shrek 2</a></i> (2004), <i><a href="/wiki/Ice_Age:_Dawn_of_the_Dinosaurs" title="Ice Age: Dawn of the Dinosaurs">Ice Age: Dawn of the Dinosaurs</a></i> (2009), and <i><a href="/wiki/Toy_Story_3" title="Toy Story 3">Toy Story 3</a></i> (2010) prior to the 2011 re-release. With the earnings of the 3D run, <i>The Lion King</i> surpassed all the aforementioned films but <i>Toy Story 3</i> to rank as the second-highest-grossing animated film worldwide—later downgraded to sixth after <i>Frozen</i> (2013), <i><a href="/wiki/Minions_(film)" title="Minions (film)">Minions</a></i> (2015), and <i><a href="/wiki/Zootopia" title="Zootopia">Zootopia</a></i> (2016)—and it remains the highest-grossing hand-drawn animated film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crown_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-crown-56">[56]</a></sup> It is also the biggest animated movie of the last 50 years in terms of estimated attendance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_theatrical_run">Original theatrical run</span></h4>
<p><i>The Lion King</i> had a limited release in North America on June 15, 1994, playing in only two theaters, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Capitan_Theater" title="El Capitan Theater">El Capitan Theater</a> in Los Angeles and <a href="/wiki/Radio_City_Music_Hall" title="Radio City Music Hall">Radio City Music Hall</a> in New York City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> It still earned $1,586,753 across the weekend of June 17–19, standing at the tenth place of the box office ranking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The average of $793,377 per theater stands as the largest ever achieved during a weekend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The wide release followed on June 24, 1994, in 2,550 screens. The digital <a href="/wiki/Surround_sound" title="Surround sound">surround sound</a> of the film led many of those theaters to implement <a href="/wiki/Dolby_Laboratories" title="Dolby Laboratories">Dolby Laboratories</a>' newest sound systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> <i>The Lion King</i> grossed $40.9 million – which at the time was the fourth biggest opening weekend earning ever and the highest sum for a Disney film  – to top the weekend box office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mane_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-mane-11">[11]</a></sup> It also earned a rare "A+" rating from <a href="/wiki/CinemaScore" title="CinemaScore">CinemaScore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> By the end of its theatrical run, in spring 1995, it had earned $312,855,561,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mojo_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-mojo-2">[2]</a></sup> being the second-highest-grossing 1994 film in North America behind <i><a href="/wiki/Forrest_Gump" title="Forrest Gump">Forrest Gump</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-movies_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-movies-63">[63]</a></sup> Outside North America, it earned $455.8 million during its initial run, for a worldwide total of $768.6 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lion-initial_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-lion-initial-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Box_Office_Mojo" title="Box Office Mojo">Box Office Mojo</a> estimates that the film sold over 74 million tickets in the US in its initial theatrical run.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-releases">Re-releases</span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="IMAX_and_large-format">IMAX and large-format</span></h5>
<p>The film was re-issued on December 25, 2002 for <a href="/wiki/IMAX" title="IMAX">IMAX</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-format" title="Large-format">large-format</a> theaters. Don Hahn explained that eight years after <i>The Lion King</i> got its original release, "there was a whole new generation of kids who haven't really seen it, particularly on the big screen." Given the film had already been digitally archived during production, the restoration process was easier, while also providing many scenes with enhancements that covered up original deficiencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70mm_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-70mm-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> An enhanced sound mix was also provided, to as Hahn explained, "make the audience feel like they're in the middle of the movie."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70mm_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-70mm-34">[34]</a></sup> On its first weekend, <i>The Lion King</i> made $2.7 million from 66 locations, a $27,664 per theater average. This run ended with $15,686,215 on May 30, 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="3D_conversion">3D conversion</span></h5>
<p>In 2011, <i>The Lion King</i> was converted to <a href="/wiki/3D_film" title="3D film">3D</a> for a two-week limited theatrical re-issue and subsequent 3D <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray</a> release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR_News_Wire_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-PR_News_Wire-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Hollywood_Reporter_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Hollywood_Reporter-67">[67]</a></sup> The film opened at the number one spot on Friday, September 16, 2011 with $8.9 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and finished the weekend with $30.2 million, ranking number one at the box office. This made <i>The Lion King</i> the first re-issue release to earn the number-one slot at the American weekend box office since the re-issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Return_of_the_Jedi" title="Return of the Jedi">Return of the Jedi</a></i> in March 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crown_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-crown-56">[56]</a></sup> The film also achieved the fourth-highest September opening weekend of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> It held off very well on its second weekend, again earning first place at the box office with a 27 percent decline to $21.9 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-70">[70]</a></sup> Most box-office observers had expected the film to fall about 50 percent in its second weekend and were also expecting <i><a href="/wiki/Moneyball_(film)" title="Moneyball (film)">Moneyball</a></i> to be at first place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>After its initial box-office success, many theaters decided to continue to show the film for more than two weeks, even though its 3D <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray</a> release was scheduled for two-and-a-half weeks after its theatrical release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-70">[70]</a></sup> In North America, the 3D re-release ended its run in theaters on January 12, 2012 with a gross $94,242,001. Outside North America, it earned $83,400,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The successful 3D re-release of <i>The Lion King</i> made Disney and <a href="/wiki/Pixar" title="Pixar">Pixar</a> plan 3D theatrical re-releases of <i><a href="/wiki/Beauty_and_the_Beast_(1991_film)" title="Beauty and the Beast (1991 film)">Beauty and the Beast</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Finding_Nemo" title="Finding Nemo">Finding Nemo</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Monsters,_Inc." title="Monsters, Inc.">Monsters, Inc.</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Little_Mermaid_(1989_film)" title="The Little Mermaid (1989 film)">The Little Mermaid</a></i> during 2012 and 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> However, none of the re-releases of the first three films achieved the enormous success of <i>The Lion King 3D</i> and theatrical re-release of the <i>The Little Mermaid</i> was ultimately cancelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In 2012, Ray Subers of <i>Box Office Mojo</i> wrote that the reason why the 3D version of <i>The Lion King</i> succeeded was because, "the notion of a 3D re-release was still fresh and exciting, and <i>The Lion King (3D)</i> felt timely given the movie's imminent Blu-ray release. Audiences have been hit with three 3D re-releases in the year since, meaning the novelty value has definitely worn off."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_response">Critical response <span id="Critics"></span></span></h3>
<p><i>The Lion King</i> was released to critical acclaim. On <a href="/wiki/Rotten_Tomatoes" title="Rotten Tomatoes">Rotten Tomatoes</a>, the film holds a rating of 91%, based on 113 reviews, with an average rating of 8.3 out of 10. It also ranked 56th on their "Top 100 Animation Movies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The site's critical consensus reads, "Emotionally stirring, richly drawn, and beautifully animated, <i>The Lion King</i> stands tall within Disney's pantheon of classic family films."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a>, the film has a score of 83 out of 100, based on 14 critics, indicating "universal acclaim".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meta_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-meta-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/CinemaScore" title="CinemaScore">CinemaScore</a> reported that audiences gave the film a rare "A+" grade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Roger_Ebert" title="Roger Ebert">Roger Ebert</a> gave it 3 1/2 out of 4-stars and called the film "a superbly drawn animated feature" and, in his print review wrote, "The saga of Simba, which in its deeply buried origins owes something to Greek tragedy and certainly to <i>Hamlet</i>, is a learning experience as well as an entertainment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tlkebertreview_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-tlkebertreview-80">[80]</a></sup> On the television program <i><a href="/wiki/At_the_Movies_(U.S._TV_series)" title="At the Movies (U.S. TV series)">Siskel &amp; Ebert</a></i>, the film was praised but received a mixed reaction when compared to previous Disney films. Ebert and his partner <a href="/wiki/Gene_Siskel" title="Gene Siskel">Gene Siskel</a> both gave the film a "Thumbs Up" but Siskel said that it was not as good as earlier films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Beauty_and_the_Beast_(1991_film)" title="Beauty and the Beast (1991 film)">Beauty and the Beast</a></i> and was "a good film, not a great one".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Hal Hinson of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> called it "an impressive, almost daunting achievement" and felt that the film was "spectacular in a manner that has nearly become commonplace with Disney's feature-length animations", but was less enthusiastic toward the end of his review saying, "Shakespearean in tone, epic in scope, it seems more appropriate for grown-ups than for kids. If truth be told, even for adults it is downright strange."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washingtonpost_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Owen_Gleiberman" title="Owen Gleiberman">Owen Gleiberman</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly" title="Entertainment Weekly">Entertainment Weekly</a></i> praised the film, writing that it "has the resonance to stand not just as a terrific cartoon but as an emotionally pungent movie".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> film critic <a href="/wiki/Peter_Travers" title="Peter Travers">Peter Travers</a> praised the film and felt that it was "a hugely entertaining blend of music, fun and eye-popping thrills, though it doesn't lack for heart".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Berardinelli" title="James Berardinelli">James Berardinelli</a> from ReelViews praised the film saying, "With each new animated release, Disney seems to be expanding its already-broad horizons a little more. <i>The Lion King</i> is the most mature (in more than one sense) of these films, and there clearly has been a conscious effort to please adults as much as children. Happily, for those of us who generally stay far away from 'cartoons', they have succeeded."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some reviewers still had problems with the film's narrative. The staff of <i><a href="/wiki/TV_Guide" title="TV Guide">TV Guide</a></i> wrote that while <i>The Lion King</i> was technically proficient and entertaining, it "offers a less memorable song score than did the previous hits, and a hasty, unsatisfying dramatic resolution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a>'</i>s Terrence Rafferty considered that despite the good animation, the story felt like "manipulat[ing] our responses at will", as "Between traumas, the movie serves up soothingly banal musical numbers and silly, rambunctious comedy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accolades">Accolades</span></h3>
<p><i>The Lion King</i> received four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Globe" title="Golden Globe">Golden Globe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> nominations. The film would go on to win two Golden Globes; for <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award_for_Best_Motion_Picture_%E2%80%93_Musical_or_Comedy" title="Golden Globe Award for Best Motion Picture – Musical or Comedy">Best Motion Picture – Musical or Comedy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award_for_Best_Original_Score" title="Golden Globe Award for Best Original Score">Best Original Score</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> as well as two Academy Awards, for <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Score" title="Academy Award for Best Original Score">Best Original Score</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hans_Zimmer" title="Hans Zimmer">Hans Zimmer</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Song" title="Academy Award for Best Original Song">Best Original Song</a> with "<a href="/wiki/Can_You_Feel_the_Love_Tonight" title="Can You Feel the Love Tonight">Can You Feel the Love Tonight</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tim_Rice" title="Tim Rice">Tim Rice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oscars_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-oscars-89">[89]</a></sup> The songs "<a href="/wiki/Circle_of_Life" title="Circle of Life">Circle of Life</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Hakuna_Matata_(song)" title="Hakuna Matata (song)">Hakuna Matata</a>" were also nominated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oscars_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-oscars-89">[89]</a></sup> "Can You Feel the Love Tonight" also won the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Award</a> for Best Male Vocal Performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <i>The Lion King</i> also won <a href="/wiki/Annie_Award" title="Annie Award">Annie Awards</a> for <a href="/wiki/Annie_Award_for_Best_Animated_Feature" title="Annie Award for Best Animated Feature">Best Animated Feature</a>, Best Achievement in Voice Acting (for <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Irons" title="Jeremy Irons">Jeremy Irons</a>) and Best Individual Achievement for Story Contribution in the Field of Animation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_Awards" title="Saturn Awards">Saturn Awards</a>, the film was nominated in two categories, Best Fantasy Film and Best Performance by a Younger Actor although it did not win in either category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The film also received two nominations at the <a href="/wiki/British_Academy_Film_Awards" title="British Academy Film Awards">British Academy Film Awards</a>, for Best Sound as well as the Anthony Asquith Award for Film Music although it lost in both categories to <i><a href="/wiki/Speed_(1994_film)" title="Speed (1994 film)">Speed</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Backbeat_(film)" title="Backbeat (film)">Backbeat</a></i> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The film received two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broadcast_Music_Incorporated" title="Broadcast Music Incorporated">BMI Film &amp; TV Awards</a> for Film Music and Most Performed Song with "Can You Feel the Love Tonight."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/1995_MTV_Movie_Awards" title="1995 MTV Movie Awards">1995 MTV Movie Awards</a>, the film received nominations for Best Villain and Best Song, though it lost in both categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <i>The Lion King</i> won the Kids' Choice Award for Favorite Movie at the <a href="/wiki/1995_Kids%27_Choice_Awards" title="1995 Kids' Choice Awards">1995 Kids' Choice Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008, <i>The Lion King</i> was ranked as the 319th greatest film ever made by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empire_(magazine)" title="Empire (magazine)">Empire</a></i> magazine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Empire5_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Empire5-97">[97]</a></sup> and in June 2011, <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)"><i>TIME</i></a> named it one of "The 25 All-TIME Best Animated Films".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In June 2008, the <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">American Film Institute</a> listed <i>The Lion King</i> as the fourth best film in the animation genre in its <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_10_Top_10" title="AFI's 10 Top 10">AFI's 10 Top 10</a> list,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> having previously put "Hakuna Matata" as 99th on its <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years...100_Songs" title="AFI's 100 Years...100 Songs">AFI's 100 Years...100 Songs</a> ranking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, the film was selected for preservation in the United States <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Registry" title="National Film Registry">National Film Registry</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> as being "culturally, historically, or aesthetically significant".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span></h3>
<p>Certain elements of the film were considered to bear a resemblance to a 1960s Japanese <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> television show, <i><a href="/wiki/Kimba_the_White_Lion" title="Kimba the White Lion">Jungle Emperor</a></i> (known as <i>Kimba the White Lion</i> in the United States), with characters having similar analogues, and various individual scenes being similar in composition to the show. Matthew Broderick believed initially that he was, in fact, working on an American version of <i>Kimba</i> since he was familiar with the Japanese original.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> However, <i>The Lion King</i> director, Roger Allers, claimed he was unfamiliar with the show:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The whole time I worked on <i>The Lion King</i> the name of that show never came up. At least I never heard it. I had never seen the show and really only became aware of it as <i>Lion King</i> was being completed, and someone showed me images of it. I worked with George Scribner and Linda Woolverton to develop the story in the early days but then left to help out on <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_(1992_Disney_film)" title="Aladdin (1992 Disney film)">Aladdin</a></i>. If one of them were familiar with Kimba they didn’t say. Of course, it’s possible... Many story ideas developed and changed along the way, always just to make our story stronger. I could certainly understand Kimba’s creators feeling angry if they felt we had stolen ideas from them. If I had been inspired by Kimba I would certainly acknowledge my inspiration. All I can offer is my respect to those artists and say that their creation has its loyal admirers and its assured place in animation history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Co-director Rob Minkoff also claimed he was unfamiliar with it "I know for a fact that ["Kimba"] has never been discussed as long as I've been on the project... In my experience, if Disney becomes aware of anything like that, they say you will not do it. People are claiming copyright infringement all the time." He also stated that whenever a story is based in Africa, it is "not unusual to have characters like a baboon, a bird or hyenas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Yoshihiro Shimizu, of <a href="/wiki/Tezuka_Productions" title="Tezuka Productions">Tezuka Productions</a>, which created <i>Kimba the White Lion</i>, has refuted rumours that the studio was paid hush money by Disney but explains that they rejected urges from within the industry to sue because, "we're a small, weak company. It wouldn't be worth it anyway ... Disney's lawyers are among the top twenty in the world!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lionkg2.jpg"><img alt='Simba lays down on a cliff, while the airborne dust next to him resembles the shape "SFX".' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="320" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1f/Lionkg2.jpg/220px-Lionkg2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/1f/Lionkg2.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lionkg2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The alleged "SEX" frame.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Protests were raised against one scene where it appears as if the word "SEX" might have been embedded into the dust flying in the sky when Simba flops down,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-The-105">[105]</a></sup> which conservative activist <a href="/wiki/Donald_Wildmon" title="Donald Wildmon">Donald Wildmon</a> asserted was a <a href="/wiki/Subliminal_stimuli" title="Subliminal stimuli">subliminal message</a> intended to promote <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexual_promiscuity" title="Sexual promiscuity">sexual promiscuity</a>. One of the animators, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Sito" title="Tom Sito">Tom Sito</a>, has stated that the letters spell "SFX" (a common abbreviation for "<a href="/wiki/Special_effect" title="Special effect">special effects</a>"), not with an "E" instead of the "F", and were intended as an innocent "signature" created by the effects animation team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
